<div id="review_10235216" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10235216" />
    <a title="Nora Dillonovich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/623295-nora-dillonovich"><img alt="Nora Dillonovich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241475277p2/623295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10235216?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nora Dillonovich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nora Dillonovich" href="/user/show/623295-nora-dillonovich">Nora Dillonovich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">tout le monde</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/623295-nora-dillonovich?shelf=imaginary-bffs-live-in-these-pages">imaginary-bffs-live-in-these-pages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/623295-nora-dillonovich?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10235216" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14909598705191258750">In the spirit of my rambling memoir/ book reviews I will begin with a childhood anecdote that somehow connects or correlates or resonates or slaps a high five with this book. <br/><br/>I was raised by a sugar-free bran loving mother. No soda ("You're better off drinking pool water Nora! Here, Christ, take a straw; go out and drink pool water if you're so intent on poisoning yourself.") No white bread (again, a reference to cholorine or bleach- some sort of chemical that would rot and/ or sicken my small i</span>
  <span id="freeText14909598705191258750" style="display:none">In the spirit of my rambling memoir/ book reviews I will begin with a childhood anecdote that somehow connects or correlates or resonates or slaps a high five with this book. <br /><br />I was raised by a sugar-free bran loving mother. No soda ("You're better off drinking pool water Nora! Here, Christ, take a straw; go out and drink pool water if you're so intent on poisoning yourself.") No white bread (again, a reference to cholorine or bleach- some sort of chemical that would rot and/ or sicken my small insides). Because of this oat bran regime I became a sugar fiend at age three (carob didn't cut it, nor did occasional nutella oatbran toasts). I used every last nickel and dime - I begged borrowed or pilfered- I recycled and cleaned couches- all in the name of candy. Glorious candy. Over time I developed a ritualistic habit of eating particular types of candy. For the most part the ceremony involved Skittles and M&amp;M's though Mike and Ikes and Starburst worked well enough. While my younger brother drank his treats down, I sat patiently, criss cross on the floor, and slid each candy coated jewel from it's pouch onto the ground before me. And then I began. With great calm and focus I silently arranged the sugary pearls- I organized, designed, and created small scale works of mathematical genius. Gingery, I slid and placed and replaced the spheres. Following my vision I arranged the mandalas, carefully eating just one orb at a time, re-arranging to compensate as sugar rounds were sacrificed to satisfy my cravings. This ritual lasted well over an hour... much to the dismay of my still not sated, impatient brother. <br /><br />Somehow, reading this book triggered that memory, and the only explanation I can come up with is that Schulz' stories, each one, is as wonderful to me now as a bag of Skittles was twenty some years ago... I desperately wanted each chapter to last- I read whole paragraphs and pages over and over again, wanting the imagery evoked to remain firmly planted in my mind. I wanted to weigh it down with some sort of ballast as lately little stays for all that long up there without a modicum of obsession and a striking amount of effort. I was like a skipping record player with some sentences, there were momentary mantras and autistic mumblings. Each story, though they are all linked, was like the most masterful sugar structure, the most precariously balanced, breath taking M&amp;M monument.<br /><br />Shulz' father, Jacob, is hands down the most fascinating character I have encountered on the page in some time. His relationships with birds, with cockroaches, with women, with fabric, are amazing. Jacob is my ideal client... I only want to work in a clinic filled with Jacob Schulzes and deformed birds. There will of course be plenty of shelving for all to climb and perch upon. <br /><br />Schulz writes like no one I have read before... his imagination seems so pure, so untainted or constricted... I don't know what else to say. Andiamo! To the bookstore my bella faccias!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14909598705191258750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2171316473" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2171316473" />
    <a title="Forrest" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811928-forrest"><img alt="Forrest" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558621304p2/811928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2171316473?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Forrest" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Forrest" href="/user/show/811928-forrest">Forrest</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2171316473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14147219986645767111">Reading Schulz's work is like discovering my newest, best literary friend. Bruno, I wish you had lived longer, though your tragic end might have been merciful, given the later alternatives. It was a strange end to an author of strange work.<br/><br/><i>The Street of Crocodiles</i> is a fever dream. It is the exposure of the bizarre from behind the curtain of what is "proper". The setting here is every bit as much of a character as the humans, dogs, and birds we come to know:<br/><br/><i>After we passed a few more houses, the</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14147219986645767111" style="display:none">Reading Schulz's work is like discovering my newest, best literary friend. Bruno, I wish you had lived longer, though your tragic end might have been merciful, given the later alternatives. It was a strange end to an author of strange work.<br /><br /><i>The Street of Crocodiles</i> is a fever dream. It is the exposure of the bizarre from behind the curtain of what is "proper". The setting here is every bit as much of a character as the humans, dogs, and birds we come to know:<br /><br /><i>After we passed a few more houses, the street ceased to maintain any pretense of urbanity, like a man returning to his little village who, piece by piece, strips off his Sunday best, slowly changing back into a peasant as he gets closer to his home.</i><br /><br />This abandonment of pretense is a running theme throughout these vignettes. Civility is continually stripped away to reveal the ugly, beautiful, rotting, shining underneath. Is it any wonder that the <a href="http://forrestaguirre.blogspot.com/2017/07/the-quay-brothers-universum.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Brothers Quay</a> did a cinematic version of <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=UIGEjEu0bn4" rel="nofollow noopener">The Street of Crocodiles</a>?<br /><br />Take, for example, the account of madness setting into a narrator's father:<br /><br /><i>Then again came days of quiet, concentrated work, interrupted by lonely monologues. While he sat there in the light of the lamp among the pillows of the large bed, and the room grew enormous as the shadows above the lampshade merged with the deep city night beyond the windows, he felt, without looking, how the pullulating jungle of wallpaper, filled with whispers, lisping and hissing, closed in around him. He heard, without looking a conspiracy of knowingly winking hidden eyes, of alert ears opening up among the flowers on the wall, of dark, smiling mouths.<br /><br />He then pretended to become even more engrossed in his work, adding and calculating, trying not to betray the anger which rose in him and overcoming the temptation to throw himself blindly forward with a sudden shout to grab fistfuls of those curly arabesques, or of those sheaves of eyes and ears which swarmed out from the night and grew and multiplied, sprouting, with ever-new ghostlike shoots and branches, from the womb of darkness.</i><br /><br />But Schulz is not only able to paint a wonderful visual picture again and again; he also has a keen gift for evocation by allusion, as when he describes one of his characters, Charles, meditating:<br /><br /><i>One of his eyes would then slightly squint to the outside, as if leaving for another dimension</i>. <br /><br />Brilliant. If I knew nothing else about this character, this one line speaks volumes about Charles' motivations and inner life, while causing me to be instantly suspicious, as well as fascinated, by this one strange tic.<br /><br />At one step of abstraction further, we must note that Schulz not only provides mood, he describes mood in a way that draws the reader in, or, rather, infects the reader in the mind's eye:<br /><br /><i>In an atmosphere of excessive facility, every whim flies high, a passing excitement swells into an empty parasitic growth; a light gray vegetation of fluffy weeds, of colorless poppies sprouts forth, made from a weightless fabric of nightmares and hashish.</i><br /><br />In a word, his work is incredible. Schulz will take you to the extremes of exhilaration and debilitating depression. His work fascinates and enthralls, like a dream from which one cannot awaken. Even in its darkest moments, I would not want to awaken from such an awe-inspiring literary dream. The "weightless fabric of nightmares and hashish," indeed!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14147219986645767111" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1656845347" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1656845347" />
    <a title="Cody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48027559-cody"><img alt="Cody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507665393p2/48027559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1656845347?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cody" href="/user/show/48027559-cody">Cody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Casebound " href="/book/show/6388757-the-street-of-crocodiles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48027559-cody?shelf=immortal">immortal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1656845347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11661309518464996749">This book. <i>This</i> goddamn book. <br/><br/><i>The Street of Crocodiles</i> tore threw me like electricity. Or enchiladas. Or electric enchiladas. You get the picture. It is so painfully lovely, so exquisitely wrought, that you have to read it to believe the defying of gravity that Schulz accomplishes here. This rare astronaut; this martyred martian. The most immediate comparison (which I don’t know why I feel compelled to make, other than with hope that it compels someone to read it) is Calvino’s <i>Cities</i>. In terms o</span>
  <span id="freeText11661309518464996749" style="display:none">This book. <i>This</i> goddamn book. <br /><br /><i>The Street of Crocodiles</i> tore threw me like electricity. Or enchiladas. Or electric enchiladas. You get the picture. It is so painfully lovely, so exquisitely wrought, that you have to read it to believe the defying of gravity that Schulz accomplishes here. This rare astronaut; this martyred martian. The most immediate comparison (which I don’t know why I feel compelled to make, other than with hope that it compels someone to read it) is Calvino’s <i>Cities</i>. In terms of plot, there’s zilch in terms of similarity. They both, however, haven’t a single word out of place and each sentence seems as much a leap of faith as something that sprang from the head of a human being—a pen-clutching Athena. You can also open either to a random chapter and be transported <i>in toto</i> to a morphing world in that fantastical DMZ between beauty and madness. The principal difference—and the reason that I do Schulz a disservice—is because this book has authentic pain throughout. <br /><br />Excuse me while I whip out my superlatives: effusive, brilliant, gorgeous, prodigious extraordinariness, virtuosic canonical genius, &amp;c. There aren’t enough words to throw at this slim volume to even begin to hint at the grandeur inside. So…I’ll let it speak where I fail:<br /><br /><i>“From that faded distance of the periphery, the city rose and grew toward the center of the map, an undifferentiated mass at first, a dense complex of blocks and houses, cut by deep canyons of streets, to become on the first plan a group of single houses, etched with the sharp clarity of a landscape seen through binoculars. In that section of the map, the engraver concentrated on the complicated and manifold profusion of streets and alleyways, the sharp lines of cornices, architraves, archivolts, and pilasters, lit by the dark gold of a late and cloudy afternoon which steeped all corners and recesses in the deep sepia of shade. The solids and prisms of that shade darkly honeycombed the ravines of streets, drowning in a warm color here half a street, there a gap between houses. They dramatized and orchestrated in a bleak romantic chiaroscuro the complex architectural polyphony.</i>”<br /><br />This is the first book of the year that I will reread before it is over. It joins the ranks of my immortal loves. For however long I’m lucky enough to convert oxygen into CO2 for plants, it will never be far from my side. If all of this sounds overly precious and gushing, trust me: <i>The Street of Crocodiles</i> deserves everything I have and more. Bless you, Bruno, bless you. I kiss your eyes in the graveyard hidden beneath the earth of Europe’s shame. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11661309518464996749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3227966098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6923392686435329920">Do Furniture and wallpaper have life? Are they in permanent chaos? Are they subject to the workings of systems and so with that are as entropic as all other systems? Do Demiurges treat tailors dummies no different than empty rooms? Can a man turn into the rubber tube of an enema? <br/>In dementia can wise questions come. Tailors Dummy is a work of genius. <br/><br/>So is Birds. Cinnamon Shops and The Comet come so close. <br/>But I don’t feel much different than I did about Sanatorium Under the Sign of the Hourglas</span>
  <span id="freeText6923392686435329920" style="display:none">Do Furniture and wallpaper have life? Are they in permanent chaos? Are they subject to the workings of systems and so with that are as entropic as all other systems? Do Demiurges treat tailors dummies no different than empty rooms? Can a man turn into the rubber tube of an enema? <br />In dementia can wise questions come. Tailors Dummy is a work of genius. <br /><br />So is Birds. Cinnamon Shops and The Comet come so close. <br />But I don’t feel much different than I did about Sanatorium Under the Sign of the Hourglass. I wrote that that was “ ....a heady mix of the metaphor with childlike fantasy and delirious dreaming that seemingly mixes the authors life memories/observations that cover his childhood through to the fear of old age and all the trials and tribulations in between.” And for me so is this, the more famous of the authors only two surviving books. All very weird and metaphorical that reaches amazing heights and then has me going Huh? What happened there?</span>
  <a data-text-id="6923392686435329920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_409110031" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/409110031" />
    <a title="Ema" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5746897-ema"><img alt="Ema" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394635581p2/5746897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/409110031?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ema" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ema" href="/user/show/5746897-ema">Ema</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/16301061-the-street-of-crocodiles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=magical-and-surreal">magical-and-surreal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=bookish-gems">bookish-gems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=favs">favs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=poetry-or-poetry-like">poetry-or-poetry-like</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=poland">poland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409110031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16356089736510742966">When I think about Bruno Schulz' life story, I always feel a pang in my heart. I'm known for my displays of pity regarding every living being, even trees (several nights ago, after a big storm, I found a young tree that was bent and was probably going to be cut down; I felt so sorry for it that went out, straightened it and tied it). So it's no surprise that the unjust death of Schulz and the disappearance of his other writing provokes a dull ache in my heart, especially after having an insight </span>
  <span id="freeText16356089736510742966" style="display:none">When I think about Bruno Schulz' life story, I always feel a pang in my heart. I'm known for my displays of pity regarding every living being, even trees (several nights ago, after a big storm, I found a young tree that was bent and was probably going to be cut down; I felt so sorry for it that went out, straightened it and tied it). So it's no surprise that the unjust death of Schulz and the disappearance of his other writing provokes a dull ache in my heart, especially after having an insight into his wonderful mind, through <i>The Street of Crocodiles</i>.<br /> <br />Reading about him, I've found out that he had no apparent influence from other writers. He lived in Drogobych all his life, being a recluse; he worked as a teacher of drawing and had a few friends, who had no idea of his literary aspirations. It was a lucky chance that one of his pen friends encouraged him to write - it was in these letters that his stories were shaped. <br /><blockquote>When Bruno Schulz's stories were reissued in Poland in 1957, translated into French and German, and acclaimed everywhere by a new generation of readers to whom he was unknown, attempts were made to place his oeuvre in the mainstream of Polish literature, to find affinities, derivations, to explain him in terms of one literary theory or another. The task is well nigh impossible. He was a solitary man, living apart, filled with his dreams, with memories of his childhood, with an intense, formidable inner life, a painter's imagination, a sensuality and responsiveness to physical stimuli which most probably could find satisfaction only in artistic creation — a volcano, smoldering silently in the isolation of a sleepy provincial town.</blockquote><br />He must have had access to some unearthly world, full of rich wonderful things, that we, normal mortals, don't have the chance to get a glimpse of, other than through the writing of Bruno Schulz and gifted writers like him. <br /><br />Reading <i>The Street of Crocodiles</i> was like having a brain orgasm: its rich metaphors were incessantly uplifting, its words caressed my mind without ever pausing. I was constantly marveling at the poetry of Schulz' prose. This could get tiring at times, because his stories never let me breath and slack, but kept requiring my ever-present attention. The task was made even more difficult because I've read the English translation and bumped into a lot of words that I didn't know, far more than in any other book I've read so far.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>After tidying up, Adela would plunge the rooms into semidarkness by drawing down the linen blinds. All colors immediately fell an octave lower, the room filled with shadows, as if it had sunk to the bottom of the sea and the light was reflected in mirrors of green water — and the heat of the day began to breathe on the blinds as they stirred slightly in their daydreams. [...] Everyone in this golden day wore that grimace of heat — as if the sun had forced his worshipers to wear identical masks of gold.</i>
</blockquote><br />Bruno Schulz is drawing inspiration from his childhood, but his writing leaves the reality at some point and wanders into imagination, blossoming into an explosion of smells and rich imagery. In the center of the stories lies Schulz' father, an almost surreal character, who gradually acquires the traits of a mythological being, wandering freely from reality into the fantastical realm. It reminded me of Danilo Kiš' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/217984.Garden__Ashes" title="Garden, Ashes by Danilo Kiš" rel="nofollow noopener">Garden, Ashes</a>, where the father was also the central, elusive figure. <br /><br />Bruno's father is shaped as an eccentric, an almost lunatic, constantly taking an interest in peculiar things, such as the long-forgotten rooms where mold and dust had settled, creating a throbbing world that disappears the moment one opens the door. Or the tailor's dummy that is not simply an object, but <i>imprisoned matter, tortured matter which does not know what it is and why it is, nor where the gesture may lead that has been imposed on it for ever</i>. He is disgusted but also fascinated by cockroaches, imitating them to the point of becoming one (this is one of his resemblances to Kafka - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/485894.The_Metamorphosis" title="The Metamorphosis by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Metamorphosis</a> - although some critics say that Schulz was not actually inspired by the first). <br /><br />The father is presented on the brink of immediate death, but he always resurfaces with renewed energy, captivating the house once more, disturbing the peace and quiet with his follies. He is the antidote for boredom, in those days that <i>hardened with cold and boredom like last year's loaves of bread. One began to cut them with blunt knives without appetite, with a lazy indifference.</i><br />At some point, the child is even convinced that his father had metamorphosed into a stuffed condor, echoing the absolutely gorgeous recount of the latter's passion for exotic birds, which were raised in the attic - one of my favorite parts of the book.<br /><br />Not only the father, but also the outer world is presented in a poetic manner, bordering on surreal: the rooms with their shape-shifting wallpapers, the garden with lush vegetation, the end-of-the-world storm, the family shop with oceans and rivers of cloth, the cardboard town from the Street of Crocodiles, the alluring Cinnamon Shops. Everything in this book is a feast of senses and I don't have enough words to praise it.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Perhaps the city and the marketplace had ceased to exist, and the gale and the night had surrounded our house with dark stage props and some machinery to imitate the howling, whistling and groaning... We were increasingly inclined to think that the gale was only an invention of the night, a poor representation on a confined stage of the tragic immensity, the cosmic homelessness and loneliness of the wind.</i>
</blockquote> <br />You should definitely read <i>The Street of Crocodiles</i>, it is truly a literary gem.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16356089736510742966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_887107890" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/887107890" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/887107890?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Jayson Fajardo</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2008-2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=polish">polish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer887107890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10128494645994125053">I was led to this book by Cynthia Ozick because in her 501 book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/162944.The_Messiah_of_Stockholm' title='The Messiah of Stockholm by Cynthia Ozick' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Messiah of Stockholm</a> (3 stars), she has a protagonist named Lars Andeming who thinks that he is the son of <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bruno_Schulz' rel='nofollow noopener'> Bruno Schulz (1892-1942).</a> So when I googled Schulz, I saw that he has this book, <i>The Street of Crocodile</i> that is included in the 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die. Bruno Schulz was a Polish writer who was finishing a novel entitled <i>Messiah</i> when he was shot by German Nazi in 1942. The manuscript has been missing sinc</span>
  <span id="freeText10128494645994125053" style="display:none">I was led to this book by Cynthia Ozick because in her 501 book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/162944.The_Messiah_of_Stockholm" title="The Messiah of Stockholm by Cynthia Ozick" rel="nofollow noopener">The Messiah of Stockholm</a> (3 stars), she has a protagonist named Lars Andeming who thinks that he is the son of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bruno_Schulz" rel="nofollow noopener"> Bruno Schulz (1892-1942).</a> So when I googled Schulz, I saw that he has this book, <i>The Street of Crocodile</i> that is included in the 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die. Bruno Schulz was a Polish writer who was finishing a novel entitled <i>Messiah</i> when he was shot by German Nazi in 1942. The manuscript has been missing since then and that was the springboard that Ozick used in her 501 novel.<br /><br /><i>The Street of Crocodiles</i> is a semi-autobiographical sketch of Schulz's life in Drohobych, Poland. It is a post-modernist fragmented novel with a distinguishable but structureless plot. When Schulz was 44 years old, he helped his fiancee in translating Franz Kafka's opus, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690.The_Trial" title="The Trial by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Trial</a> (3 stars). This book was first published as <i>The Cinnamon Shops</i> in 1934, 9 years after <i>The Trial</i> was first published. Obviously, Kafka was one of the major influences in Schulz writing and it is apparent in this book by having the kafkaesque elements of bizarre, meaningless, hopelessness meaning no-escape situations. <br /><br />The chapters appear as pieces of situations that describe the main characters: the narrator's father who stays in the basement of the house and does a lot of strange things including some kind of experiments; the house maid <b>Adela</b> who acts like a prima donna; the narrator's <b>Uncle Edward</b> who is estranged to his father but the narrator admires; and a handful of other minor characters. It could be about many things but my take is that it is a story of a boy's relationship with a strange father (who turns to a cockroach like Gregor Samsa). However, beyond all of the possible interpretations, what is most striking in Schultz's writing is his vivid and detailed description of his locales (settings). Described in his prose sounding like poetry, the books is a riveting read as you will be enthralled by the beauty of Schulz's words.<br /><br />Easy short novella with mesmerizing prose that sounds like poetry. Beautiful. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10128494645994125053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1533928623" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1533928623" />
    <a title="Antonomasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia"><img alt="Antonomasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605185148p2/6249204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1533928623?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonomasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonomasia" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia">Antonomasia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140042276" href="/book/show/917863.The_Street_of_Crocodiles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=central-eastern-europe">central-eastern-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=decade-1930s">decade-1930s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=ol">ol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=poland">poland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=jewish">jewish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1533928623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3134152085484909644">A volume of Bruno Schulz stories is like an impossibly delicious and ornate gateau; it's impossible to eat the whole thing at once. (Although this didn't take me as long to read as the dates above may suggest: it was more like a fortnight's worth of days, in two phases separated by eleven months.) But it's also not simply the feast of sweetmeats and beauty that this collection's more fitting, original Polish title, <i>Cinnamon Shops</i> may suggest: in among the glittering decorations are also, if you </span>
  <span id="freeText3134152085484909644" style="display:none">A volume of Bruno Schulz stories is like an impossibly delicious and ornate gateau; it's impossible to eat the whole thing at once. (Although this didn't take me as long to read as the dates above may suggest: it was more like a fortnight's worth of days, in two phases separated by eleven months.) But it's also not simply the feast of sweetmeats and beauty that this collection's more fitting, original Polish title, <i>Cinnamon Shops</i> may suggest: in among the glittering decorations are also, if you look more closely, alarmingly sinister, creepy gothic ones. <br /><br />A couple of years ago, I said a lot of what I'd still say about Schulz, in the second half of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1328413410" rel="nofollow noopener">this post</a>, along with some favourite quotes, in reviewing a novella about him which also reprinted a couple of his stories - and which, finally, belatedly, got me reading him. (I'd known of Schulz for a long time, but as mentioned there, had always been put off by the title <i>The Street of Crocodiles</i>, combined with the seriousness and slight sadism of the black and white illustration on the front of the recent Penguin Classics editions. I'd have gone instead for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1316033771" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a> side of his drawing style, for it's lovely combination of realism and what might now be called chibi-ness, and which matches the caricature-like, fantastical nature of his metamorphoses of family and friends into his fictional characters. Its childlike-ness is far nearer the essence of the book than the fetishy foot-in-the-face of the Penguin choice.)<br /><br />The couple of stories I'd previously read, 'Birds' and 'Cinnamon Shops', contained much of the essence of Schulz's fiction, but a few things were new to me in reading the whole <i>Street of Crocodiles</i> volume. (This was just his first collection of stories I read, not one of the editions that confusingly combines it with <i>Sanatorium Under the Hourglass</i> while still using the earlier title.) One is how Adela, the maid, is the calm centre of the household who restores balance and order - I was always so glad when she bustled into view - in contrast to the fantastically eccentric parents whose establishment it technically is. <br /><br />After reading 'Nimrod' and 'Cockroaches' I understand more about why Schulz is compared to Kafka as a writer, and not just as a personality. That Schulz came up with these two stories without having read 'Investigations of a Dog' or 'Metamorphosis' shows, I think, how each author was a lightning rod for certain features in the culture of his times and region, drawn by their similar character traits. (I don't think Kafka was so widely known, nor that mass media communications were so good, that Schulz might have picked up the essence of the stories without having read them, as one might nowadays.) 'Cockroaches' is one of a number of stories or mere sentences that seem to prefigure elements of the rise of Nazism and the Holocaust which would kill the author less than ten years after the book was published. Granted, many if not most classic novels from the 1920s and 30s contain the occasional nod to the forms of racism and eugenicism which were pervasive and prevalent at the time, and perhaps I found some of it more poignant and sad than elsewhere because of Schulz's own fate. He was not immune, for how would he necessarily know to be, to making an unflattering description of effeminate men and dark-skinned ladies (in the title story); and for a Jewish man in 1933 to have written of a member of his own family turning into a cockroach feels far more obviously and frighteningly illustrative of insults that we can readily assume existed in the streets and printed media than it does in 1915. (Yet it's not as if there wasn't also violent antisemitism then; the dark clouds of history just make one see differently.) Then there is the uncle stripped of his personality (by stringent psychoanalysis, a dig at Freudians, perhaps?) made functional, mechanical, a Taylorist's dream: into a doorbell. Perhaps most chilling were parts of 'The Comet': <i>One day my brother, on his return from school, brought the improbable and yet true news of the imminent end of the world. We asked him to repeat it, thinking that we had misheard. We hadn't... unready and unfinished, just as it was, at a random point in time and space, without closing its accounts, without having reached any goal, in mid-sentence as it were, without a period or exclamation mark, without a last judgement or God's Wrath - in an atmosphere of friendly understanding, loyally, by mutual agreement and in accordance with rules observed by both parties - the world was to be hit on the head, simply and irrevocably.</i> Several pages later (though still mid story) <i>they marked the paths of a sleepy cosmography, while, in reality, black as soot, they succumbed to a planetary lethargy, as if they had put their heads into the fireplace, the final goal of all those blind flights.</i> I cannot but hear, here, the bewildering stasis of the man who (like Hungarian Antal Szerb), depite no shortage of offers to get him and his great talent to safety, remained in his hometown as the Nazis took over and was eventually murdered as a result.<br /><br /><br />Celina Wieniewska's original translation of Schulz still appears to be the only one in print, and I can see why; it is beautiful reading in English, it still feels right alongside modern traditions of fantasy, fairytale retellings and the gothic - at least for someone who can't read the original, it seems no improvement is required - and the only thing out of place to the contemporary eye, used to new translations, is that many characters' names are anglicised.<br /><br />Despite what I may have said in the last few paragraphs…<br /><br />What this book is not: depressing Holocaust lit; cold, dry, serious East European SF (I used to think it would be, the way I imagine the Strugatskys); continually or frequently ugly; pinched and cornered and paranoid and stressful like Kafka (I don’t enjoy Kafka, sorry).<br /><br />It is: deliciously language- and metaphor-rich, it is fantastical, it is often escapist, it is fun (if your idea of fun can include the occasional bit of queasy Gothicism). Schulz is better known than he was, but there are still friends who haven’t read him whom I think would love this. He’s not for absolutely everyone, but he’s the sort of writer whose work you want to actively press on those you think would enjoy it, it’s so wonderful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3134152085484909644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3031507341" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3031507341?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Appleton" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton">Matthew Appleton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=read-2019">read-2019</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3031507341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6503485736635123920">What is reality? One of the best questions, I think, in the world. Regardless of the answer, The Street of Crocodiles takes us into the mythical and dreamlike reality of Schulz. His Polish childhood, yes, but what else! Countless bizarre happenings, impossible things with no explanations, as wonderful as Kafka. Surreal and beautiful, two words to describe the book. The translation I have is beautiful too. How I wish I could read Schulz's talent in his native tongue. <br/><br/>The 'chapters' are named as f</span>
  <span id="freeText6503485736635123920" style="display:none">What is reality? One of the best questions, I think, in the world. Regardless of the answer, The Street of Crocodiles takes us into the mythical and dreamlike reality of Schulz. His Polish childhood, yes, but what else! Countless bizarre happenings, impossible things with no explanations, as wonderful as Kafka. Surreal and beautiful, two words to describe the book. The translation I have is beautiful too. How I wish I could read Schulz's talent in his native tongue. <br /><br />The 'chapters' are named as follows in the book:<br /><br />- August<br />- Visitation<br />- Birds<br />- Tailor's Dummies<br />- Nimrod<br />- Pan<br />- Mr Charles<br />- Cinnamon Shops<br />- The Street of Crocodiles<br />- Cockroaches<br />- The Gale<br />- The Night of the Great Season<br />- The Comet<br /><br />I don't really want to spoil the mad things in the book, but I will leave some quotes here that give glimpses into the world of Schulz. <br /><br />'It was a dialogue as grim as the language of thunder. The jerkings of his arms cut the sky into pieces, and in the cracks there appeared the face of Jehovah swollen with anger and spitting out curses.'<br /><br />"Have you heard at night the terrible howling of these wax figures, shut in their fairbooths; the pitiful chorus of those forms of wood or porcelain, banging their fists against the walls of their prisons?"<br /><br />'The horse was panting, hanging its head. I hugged its head to my breast and saw that there were tears in its large eyes. I noticed a round black wound on its belly. 'Why did you not tell me?' I whispered, crying. 'My dearest, I did it for you,' the horse said and became very small, like a wooden toy. I left him and felt wonderfully light and very happy.'<br /><br />'Full of ideas and projects, I wanted to walk towards my home, but met some school friends with books under their arms. They were on their way to school already, having being wakened by the brightness of that night that would not end. <br />We went for a walk altogether along a steeply falling street, pervaded by the scent of violets; uncertain whether it was the magic of the night which lay like silver on the snow or whether it was the light of dawn...'<br /><br />'One is never sure whether the rain will come at all or where it will stop if it does. It often happens, therefore, that people wait in two different places, unable to agree where the stop is. They wait for a long time standing in a black, silent bunch alongside the barely visible lines of the track, their faces in profile: a row of pale cutout paper figures, fixed in an expression of anxious peering.<br />At last a train suddenly appears: one can see it coming from the expected side street, low like a snake, a miniature train with a squat puffing locomotive.'<br /><br />'There were among them two-headed birds and birds with many wings, there were cripples too, limping through the air in one-winged, awkward flight. The sky now resembled those old murals, full of monsters and fantastic beasts, which circled around, passing and eluding each other in elliptical manoeuvres.'</span>
  <a data-text-id="6503485736635123920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_819956865" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/819956865" />
    <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan"><img alt="Dhanaraj Rajan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433251321p2/4373783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/819956865?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dhanaraj Rajan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dhanaraj Rajan" href="/user/show/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan">Dhanaraj Rajan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=literature">literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer819956865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5550850939330159937">Few Questions and Answers: <br/><br/>1. Can a simple ordinary fact of a normal day, become a superb piece of literature? For instance, the first act of barking of a puppy - can there be anything interesting to describe it? Not to say anything of four pages of pure pleasure and in the form of a 'short story'. Schulz does it very easily.<br/><br/>2. Can language be intoxicating? The language intoxicates you and results in high voltage creations of hallucinations. An example: In the middle of the day, when the sun was</span>
  <span id="freeText5550850939330159937" style="display:none">Few Questions and Answers: <br /><br />1. Can a simple ordinary fact of a normal day, become a superb piece of literature? For instance, the first act of barking of a puppy - can there be anything interesting to describe it? Not to say anything of four pages of pure pleasure and in the form of a 'short story'. Schulz does it very easily.<br /><br />2. Can language be intoxicating? The language intoxicates you and results in high voltage creations of hallucinations. An example: In the middle of the day, when the sun was relatively shining well I was reading a narration of a wintry night from this book and at the end of my reading I was fully convinced of the book than the real climate outside of my window!<br /><br />3. Can an event be 're-lived' according to your childhood imaginations? Schulz will help you live it. What were your childhood ideas regarding the candy shops or shops with exotic items? How did you view the same shops in the night when they were filled with colourful lights? Read Schulz and you will know exactly how you had imagined them as a Child. <br /><br />4. Can a person be so simple and at the same time very profound? An apparently simple narration suddenly turns into a narration with many significance. For instance, Schulz in six or eight pages creates a story that resembles and gives the same effect as Kafka's Metamorphosis. <br /><br /><br />This book was a gift from a friend. And I am grateful to that friend for introducing Schulz. Some people truly know how to give gifts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5550850939330159937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1705159894" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1705159894" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1663300-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508879587p2/1663300.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1705159894?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/1663300-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1663300-tara?shelf=classic-fiction">classic-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1705159894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3772190176976137839">I discovered Bruno Schulz through an amazing short story by contemporary author <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5622334.Helen_Maryles_Shankman' title='Helen Maryles Shankman' rel='nofollow noopener'>Helen Maryles Shankman</a>. She writes a fictional account of the interaction between Shulz and his Nazi protector during World War II in Poland. The exquisite story, "In the Land of Armadillos," made my want to read Shulz's own work. This classic story collection is a dreamy, post-modernist, surreal walk through the streets of Drogobych and through the many rooms of Shulz's imagination. Likened to Kafka, the book as a w</span>
  <span id="freeText3772190176976137839" style="display:none">I discovered Bruno Schulz through an amazing short story by contemporary author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5622334.Helen_Maryles_Shankman" title="Helen Maryles Shankman" rel="nofollow noopener">Helen Maryles Shankman</a>. She writes a fictional account of the interaction between Shulz and his Nazi protector during World War II in Poland. The exquisite story, "In the Land of Armadillos," made my want to read Shulz's own work. This classic story collection is a dreamy, post-modernist, surreal walk through the streets of Drogobych and through the many rooms of Shulz's imagination. Likened to Kafka, the book as a whole reminded me of a very different version of Sherwood Anderson's <i>Winesburg, Ohio</i>.<br /><br />I think Shankman says it best in her own review: "Read The Street of Crocodiles if you're interested in what was lost in the fires of the Holocaust. Read it if you want to be consumed by fiction that burns like poetry. But by all means, read this book."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3772190176976137839" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109291482" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109291482" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109291482?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=mid-20th-century">mid-20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=europe-other">europe-other</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109291482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13665626743060763598">There are two introductions to this edition. One is by the translator Celine Wieniewska, and the other by Jerzy Ficowski, who must have written the introduction to the original. I don't recall in which, but one of them says this is a novel - and it is, of sorts. It is collection of inter-connected stories, a novel in the same way, perhaps, that <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1736739.Olive_Kitteridge' title='Olive Kitteridge by Elizabeth Strout' rel='nofollow noopener'>Olive Kitteridge</a> is a novel. Please do not mistake me: that inter-connectedness the only thing the two titles have in common. These stories are all told </span>
  <span id="freeText13665626743060763598" style="display:none">There are two introductions to this edition. One is by the translator Celine Wieniewska, and the other by Jerzy Ficowski, who must have written the introduction to the original. I don't recall in which, but one of them says this is a novel - and it is, of sorts. It is collection of inter-connected stories, a novel in the same way, perhaps, that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1736739.Olive_Kitteridge" title="Olive Kitteridge by Elizabeth Strout" rel="nofollow noopener">Olive Kitteridge</a> is a novel. Please do not mistake me: that inter-connectedness the only thing the two titles have in common. These stories are all told in the first person about his family - mostly his father. <br /><br />Several of these stories could be called fantasy, but not in the more contemporary sense. Schulz takes us on flights of his imagination in a fantastical way. <i>Conjecture easily changes into certainty, the obvious into illusion; possibilities </i>materialize<i>. Myth stalks the streets of Drogobych, turning ragamuffins playing tiddleywinks into enchanted soothsayers who read the future in the cracks of a wall, or transforming a shopkeeper into a prophet or a goblin.</i> (From the introduction by Jerzy Ficowski.)<br /><br />This is filled with such beautiful language! I read in another review somewhere an analogy that one could not read much at a time because it would be like sitting down to a 7-course meal of rich chocolate. I could quote from nearly every page, but here are two examples, from two different stories.<blockquote>I heard my father's voice during the intermissions in these prophetic tirades. I heard the windows shake from the powerful growl of the swollen lips, mixed with the explosions of entreaties, laments, and threats uttered by Father.<br /><br />At the time of the shortest, sleepy winter days, edged on both sides with the furry dusk of mornings and evenings, when the city reached out deeper and deeper into the labyrinth of winter nights, and was shaken reluctantly into consciousness by the short dawn, my father was already lost, sold and surrendered to the other sphere.</blockquote>This later quote is the opening line of the story "Cinnamon Shops". This was the title of the collection when it first appeared. I don't know why it was changed for the English language collection, which are the same stories. I also liked "Tailors Dummies" which apparently is really a series of three stories. A third favorite is "The Gale" where Schulz truly lets his imagination run free. There were a couple of stories I could easily have skipped, though I didn't.<br /><br />I spent a bit over a month with this. I needed to intersperse my reading of the stories with my other reading. I think doing it that way made me appreciate this short volume that much more. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13665626743060763598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3241327492" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3241327492" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3241327492?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=czech-republic-poland">czech-republic-poland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3241327492" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8288304897710045949"><br/>"The old established inhabitants of the city kept away from that area where the scum, the lowest orders had settled—creatures without character, without background, moral dregs, that inferior species of human being which is born in such ephemeral communities. But on days of defeat, in hours of moral weakness, it would happen that one or another of the city dwellers would venture half by chance into that dubious district. The best among them were not entirely free from the temptation of voluntary</span>
  <span id="freeText8288304897710045949" style="display:none"><br />"The old established inhabitants of the city kept away from that area where the scum, the lowest orders had settled—creatures without character, without background, moral dregs, that inferior species of human being which is born in such ephemeral communities. But on days of defeat, in hours of moral weakness, it would happen that one or another of the city dwellers would venture half by chance into that dubious district. The best among them were not entirely free from the temptation of voluntary degradation, of breaking down the barriers of hierarchy, of immersion in that shallow mud of companionship, of easy intimacy, of dirty intermingling."<br /><br />The Street of Crocodiles had been on my radar for quite some time now and I finally got round to see if the glowing reviews for Bruno Schulz' writing really did live up to it. Simple answer is yes. Something like Proust meets Kafka would be the easiest assumption to make, but there is something in there that is undoubtedly all of Schulz' own. With a paralyzing intimacy for Drogobych, and a family and environment that completely engulfed him, he takes this real town in real time filled with real people and twists things around in a style that is both dark and hideous, beautiful and affecting—where the malevolent is deadpan, and the imagery always striking.<br />Schulz was clearly one of the most original European writers of his time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8288304897710045949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1108295482?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janet" href="/user/show/1421407-janet">Janet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1421407-janet?shelf=best-books-under-120-pages">best-books-under-120-pages</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1108295482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8970769421703967950">A strange and magical experience. The small stories in this book are language-drunk, old-world-surrealism, the emphasis on the weird life of objects, and a father who at various times turns into a bird, saves the world, forgets his wallet, pursues the maid, creates a world out of bolts of cloth, and--of course! becomes a cockroach…the Mittel-Europa city that morphs and morphs again… Only the maid never changes from story to story. A vanished world, a child's wavering sense of reality, a street o</span>
  <span id="freeText8970769421703967950" style="display:none">A strange and magical experience. The small stories in this book are language-drunk, old-world-surrealism, the emphasis on the weird life of objects, and a father who at various times turns into a bird, saves the world, forgets his wallet, pursues the maid, creates a world out of bolts of cloth, and--of course! becomes a cockroach…the Mittel-Europa city that morphs and morphs again… Only the maid never changes from story to story. A vanished world, a child's wavering sense of reality, a street of magic which cannot always be found and then appears when you're lost… it is quite the Chagall world, with a darker undertow of contagion and panic, leavened with whimsy. The stories don't seem 'finished' in any conventional sense, but explode like flash-paper before the settle to the ground in a heap of glitter. Coruscating and mysterious, they remind me at times of Fernando del Paso's word-drunk Palinuro of Mexico as much as Kafka. Another book from Les Plesko's incredible reading list, it was a favorite of his. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.pleskoism.wordpress.com">www.pleskoism.wordpress.com</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8970769421703967950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer743775892" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1431234561123592885">Profoundly weird in the best of ways. Sort of like a bizarre hybrid of Proust, Kafka, and the '30s surrealists. There are moments in here that match the best I've experienced in any book. The first collection in particular, Street of Crocodiles, is a Winesburg, Ohio style linked set of stories that does tremendous work. Bumped down a star only because for large chunks of pages my mind wandered - the experimentalist tangents can go on for way too long. But this is an accomplishment. The "if only"</span>
  <span id="freeText1431234561123592885" style="display:none">Profoundly weird in the best of ways. Sort of like a bizarre hybrid of Proust, Kafka, and the '30s surrealists. There are moments in here that match the best I've experienced in any book. The first collection in particular, Street of Crocodiles, is a Winesburg, Ohio style linked set of stories that does tremendous work. Bumped down a star only because for large chunks of pages my mind wandered - the experimentalist tangents can go on for way too long. But this is an accomplishment. The "if only" question is so high with Schulz; it's just impossible to ignore the tragedy of his biography. I hope the novel surfaces one day. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1431234561123592885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59785585-vesna?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59785585-vesna?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59785585-vesna?shelf=experimental">experimental</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59785585-vesna?shelf=magic-realism">magic-realism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3387976911" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13127192610511473276">The book title in some editions includes “… and other stories”, also suggested in the blurb, which slightly misled me to think that it’s a collection of stories. As I rarely read story collections in linear fashion, I started with a couple of the more famous “stories” which are in the second half of the book only to realize that they are interconnected as are almost all others in this fascinating and genre-bending book. It was originally published under the title of a different "story" <i>Cinnamon </i></span>
  <span id="freeText13127192610511473276" style="display:none">The book title in some editions includes “… and other stories”, also suggested in the blurb, which slightly misled me to think that it’s a collection of stories. As I rarely read story collections in linear fashion, I started with a couple of the more famous “stories” which are in the second half of the book only to realize that they are interconnected as are almost all others in this fascinating and genre-bending book. It was originally published under the title of a different "story" <i>Cinnamon Shops</i>, which turned out to be my favorite. When asked about its genre, Bruno Schulz replied: <blockquote>To what genre does <i>Cinnamon Shops [The Street of Crocodiles]</i> belong? How should it be classified? I consider it an autobiographical novel, not merely because it is written in the first person and one can recognize in it certain events and experiences from the author's own childhood. It is an autobiography—or rather, a genealogy—of the spirit…<br />(quoted in Ficowski’s introduction)</blockquote>The spirit of his family, above all his father, and the spirit of the small town in which Schulz grew up and lived run through all the “stories” and it would be best to read this book from start to finish even though there are no conventional “plots” and this certainly is not a standard novel. For Schulz takes us into a fantastic world of labyrinths of streets, labyrinths of rooms in his house, labyrinths of thoughts in his father’s mind… The line between real and mythical is often blurred, inanimate objects can behave, think or feel as living organisms, everything and everyone can at any point metamorphose into something else. This constantly shifting reality makes the magical world of Schulz’s “spiritual genealogy” an open field for different interpretations, all of which may indeed be correct as his multilayered storytelling is anything but a simple memory of one’s childhood. <br /><br />For me, at this round of reading (for this is the kind of book that invites us to continually re-read at different times), two things stood out. First there is Schulz’s beautiful prose bordering on the poetic. I would often stop to re-read sentences or passages just to marvel at their beauty and construction as if standing in front of a painted masterpiece. Then there is the fascinating character of the father, a small shop owner, who breaks away from the monotony of life in a multitude of forms. His spirit takes him into the passions and behavior that the family sees as his descent into madness. But, in Schulz’s narrative imaginations, he is also seen as a metamorphosing free spirit who runs away and occasionally stands up to the conventional order as personified in his nemesis, their housemaid Adela. He is an eccentric bird collector, a “heresiarch” (a spiritual leader of his own new “religion” called the Great Heresy), a mad scientist who turns his brother-in-law into a bellphone and much more. <br /><br />Besides Schulz’s imaginative ability to create the semi-mythical world, there is also the humor, especially in the last stories, parodying the world of social conventions and, in the last story “Comet”, technological progress for its lack of humanity and soul. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As a self-styled scientist, his father comically turns uncle Edward into a bellphone, but then later, as an inspired visionary who can see passed the technological fads and limited science instruments (though never clear if he is a madman too), his father creates no less but a new form of human life. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> There are many other themes in this book, narrated with the magical pen of a genius, and I am not surprised that Schulz influenced so many writers (I see him referred to as "a writer's writer"), and even inspired the superbly original review on Goodreads - see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1540583392" rel="nofollow noopener">Fionnuala's review</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13127192610511473276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
